    "Collection of Ancient and Modern Books: Complete Medical Volume 301"

    Paralysis and Weakness

    Huangdi's Suwen

    On the Great Discussion of the Four Seasons and Regulation of the Spirit

    In the winter month of three, this is the time for closing and storing. The water freezes and the earth cracks; do not disturb the Yang. Go to bed early and rise late, waiting for the sunlight. Make your intentions like those that are hidden and secretive, as if you possess private thoughts or already have achievements. Retreat from the cold and approach the warmth, avoiding any excessive exposure of the skin, so that the vital energy does not hastily diminish. This is the response of winter air and the method of nurturing and storing. Going against this will harm the kidneys, leading to weakness in spring, resulting in fewer offspring.

    [Note: The kidneys are associated with winter. Opposing the stored energy of winter harms the kidneys, which results in weakness in spring, thus leading to fewer offspring. The liver, representing wood, thrives in winter water and governs the energy and nourishment of spring, vital for the tendons. If the tendons lose their nourishment, weakness arises; if the vital energy descends violently, it leads to weakness.][1p]
    The Theory of Vital Energy Reaching the Heavens

    Due to dampness, the head feels wrapped; if damp heat is not expelled, the major tendons are soft and short, while the minor tendons are slack and long. Softness and shortness cause contraction, while slackness and length lead to paralysis.

    Note: The dampness obstructs the vital Yang energy, hence the head feels wrapped. When Yang energy is weak, it fails to nourish the tendons; therefore, major tendons become soft and short, while minor tendons elongate and slacken. The major tendons are connected to the minor tendons within the bones and joints, thus they become soft and short due to stagnant heat; externally, dampness causes them to be slack and long. Shortness results in spasms, while length leads to abandonment.

    The Theory of Distinctions between Yin and Yang

    When the Three Yang energies are affected, symptoms include chills and fever, abscesses and swellings, as well as paralysis and numbness.

    Note: The Three Yang refers to the Sun Yang. The Sun Yang is the master of all Yang energies and governs the tendons; when the tendons are injured, it results in paralysis, and when the energy is harmed, it leads to numbness. When the Three Yang and Three Yin are ill, it results in partial atrophy and easy paralysis, causing the limbs to be motionless.

    Note: The Three Yang and Three Yin refers to the Sun and the Greater Yin. If the Sun Yang is deficient, it leads to partial atrophy, and if Yang is deficient and fails to nourish the tendons, it results in paralysis. The spleen pertains to the limbs, thus they become immobile.[2p]
    Discussion on Atrophy

    The Yellow Emperor asked: "What causes atrophy in the five organs?" Qi Bo replied: "The lungs govern the skin and hair, the heart governs the blood vessels, the liver governs the tendons and membranes, the spleen governs the muscles, and the kidneys govern the bone marrow. Thus, when the lung heat causes dryness, the skin and hair are weakened and thin, which results in atrophy and paralysis.

    [Note: The ability of the body to move and act comes from the nourishment of the internal organs to the tendons, blood vessels, bones, and muscles. When the internal organs are diseased, the exterior of the body is atrophied. Lung heat leads to dryness and thinness of the skin, and since the skin relies on the lungs for nourishment, when the skin is thin, the essence and fluids cannot circulate, resulting in overheating of the five organs and consequently atrophy and paralysis.]

    When heart qi is hot, the lower vessels are constricted and the upper vessels are weak. When the upper is weak, there arises atrophy in the vessels, causing joints to be unsteady and the shins to be slack and unable to support the body.

    [Note: When heart qi is hot, the qi rises and causes the pulse to become constricted in the upper body. The heart governs the vessels, hence the qi of the pulse turns constricted and rises, leading to weakness in the pulses below. When the meridians are weak, the joints below become unstable; since the joints are unsteady, the tendons and bones loosen, causing the shins to become slack and unable to support the ground.][3p]
    When liver qi is heated, it causes the gallbladder to leak, resulting in a bitter mouth; the fascia becomes dry, and when the fascia is dry, the tendons become tense and cramp, leading to tendon atrophy.

    (Note: The gallbladder is attached to the liver, and when liver qi is heated, bile leaks, causing bitterness in the mouth. When bile leaks, the fascia cannot be nourished and dries out, hence the tension and cramps leading to tendon atrophy.)

    When spleen qi is heated, it leads to a dry stomach and thirst; the muscles become numb, leading to muscle atrophy.

    (Note: The qi of the spleen and stomach governs the muscles. When the yangming fluids do not produce, and the taiyin qi does not arrive, the muscles become numb and lead to muscle atrophy.)

    When kidney qi is heated, the lower back and spine cannot rise, the bones become frail, and the marrow diminishes, leading to bone atrophy.

    (Note: Kidney heat depletes fluids; the lower back is the orifice of the kidneys, therefore the lower back and spine cannot extend, leading to diminished marrow and frail bones resulting in bone atrophy.)

    The Emperor asked: How does one come to this? Qi Bo said: The lungs are the master of the viscera and cover the heart. When something is lost, and what one seeks cannot be obtained, the lungs become noisy; when they are noisy, the lung heat causes the leaves to wither. Therefore, it is said that the five viscera suffer from lung heat causing withering and paralysis—this is what it means.

    (Note: When something is lost, and what one seeks cannot be attained, the heart’s spirit becomes unsettled, and the fire qi rises.)[4p]
    The lungs are the covering of the heart. When metal (the lungs) suffers the fire’s punishment, it leads to wheezing. If the lungs become overheated and dry, there is no way for the fluids to circulate, resulting in heat affecting all five organs. Excessive sorrow can sever the channels, and when they are severed, the yang energy stirs within. This can lead to a collapse beneath the heart, manifesting as blood in the urine. Therefore, this condition is called “the great vessels are empty,” which manifests as muscle atrophy and is transmitted as pulse weakness. Endless thoughts and unfulfilled wishes lead to excessive external desires, causing the tendons to loosen and resulting in muscle atrophy and excessive lust. Thus, it is stated in the lower classic: “Muscle atrophy arises from the liver.” This is related to dampness; if moisture lingers and the environment is damp, the flesh becomes soaked, leading to numbness and resulting in flesh atrophy. Therefore, it is stated in the lower classic: “Flesh atrophy arises from damp conditions.” Long journeys leading to fatigue, encountering great heat and thirst, can internally consume the yang energy. When it is consumed, heat lodges in the kidneys, which are the water organs. If water cannot withstand fire, bones will become withered and marrow will be depleted, leading to an inability of the feet to support the body, resulting in bone atrophy. Therefore, it is stated in the lower classic: “Bone atrophy arises from great heat.”

    (Note: “Channels” here refers to the Chongmai, or Penetrating Vessel.)[5p]
    The Chong vessel originates from the center of the womb and is considered the sea of the twelve meridians. The heart governs the blood vessels; therefore, if the collateral of the womb is blocked, the heart qi becomes deficient and moves internally. When the heart qi moves, it leads to a downward flow resulting in frequent urination of blood. If there is a deficiency of blood in the exterior vessels, it leads to muscular paralysis, while a deficiency of blood in the internal vessels results in weakness of the pulse. The blood lost in urination can damage the skin and affect the qi and blood in the meridians. Thus, it is said that the great vessels are empty. Endless thoughts and unfulfilled desires can harm liver qi. Those who indulge excessively can experience intense desire leading to spontaneous emission. If there is residual dampness and one resides in a low and moist environment, the combination of internal and external dampness can cause the muscles to become soft and numb, leading to muscular atrophy. Long journeys and exhaustion can harm the kidneys; exposure to extreme heat can damage the yin, and thirst can deplete the yin fluids. Therefore, the yang heat can attack the yin, causing heat to accumulate in the kidneys. Now, with excessive yang and depleted yin, water cannot withstand fire, leading to dryness of the bones and deficiency of the marrow, resulting in inability to support oneself, causing bone atrophy. The emperor asked, "How can one differentiate these conditions?" Qi Bo replied: "Lung heat presents with pale complexion and damaged hair; heart heat shows a red complexion with engorged vessels; liver heat has a greenish hue with brittle nails; spleen heat exhibits a yellow color with twitching flesh; kidney heat appears black with decayed teeth."[6p]
    [Note: All atrophy diseases are due to heat in the five organs leading to depletion of essence, unable to nourish the tendons, channels, bones, and flesh; thus, their causes differ and should be diagnosed based on the physical appearance.] The Emperor said: As you say, it is so. Regarding the treatment of atrophy, why alone is the Yangming selected? Qibo replied: Yangming is the sea of the five organs and six bowels, governing the nourishment of the tendons and the binding of bones, which facilitate movement. The Chong vessel is the sea of channels, governing the seepage into valleys, and it connects with Yangming at the tendons. The meeting of Yin and Yang is at the tendons, gathering at the Qi thoroughfare, and Yangming presides over it; thus, they all belong to the Dai vessel and intertwine with the Governing vessel. Therefore, when Yangming is deficient, the tendons become loose, the Dai vessel does not pull, resulting in the feet being weak and unable to move. [Note: The five organs and six bowels all receive Qi from Yangming, hence it is called the sea of organs and bowels. The front Yin is where the tendons gather, combining with Taiyin and Yangming, making it the meeting point of all tendons. The blood and Qi generated by Yangming nourish it, hence all atrophies are uniquely treated through Yangming. The Chong vessel arises within the womb, ascending along the back, being the sea of channels.][7p]
    What floats externally permeates the valleys, merging with Yangming at the governing vessel. The Shaoyin, Taiyin, and Yangming channels converge at the governing vessel, following the abdomen upwards and meeting at the Qi street, which is governed by Yangming; hence, Yangming is its chief. The three Yin and three Yang, twelve primary channels, along with the extraordinary channels Ren, Du, and Chong, flow up and down, all pertained to the constraints of the Dai channel. The Du channel originates at the perineum, bifurcating into Ren and Chong, ascending along the back and abdomen; thus, Ren, Chong, Shaoyin, and Yangming are all interconnected with the Du channel. Yangming is the sea of food and fluids, governing the nourishment of the governing vessel; if deficient, the governing vessel relaxes, unable to bind the bones, leading to dysfunction in the limbs, resulting in atrophy; if the Dai channel fails to extend, the channels below slacken, causing the feet to weaken and become unusable. This explains why atrophy specifically pertains to Yangming. The Emperor asked: How can one differentiate? Qibo replied: In cases of lung heat, the complexion is pale with hair loss; in cases of heart heat, the complexion is red with engorged vessels; in cases of liver heat, the complexion is bluish with brittle nails; in cases of spleen heat, the complexion is yellow with muscle twitching; in cases of kidney heat, the complexion is dark with decayed teeth.

    (Note: The atrophy condition is caused by heat in the five organs leading to the depletion of essences unable to nourish the channels, bones, and flesh; therefore, the signs differ, and should all be diagnosed based on appearance and color.)[8p]
    The emperor said: "As you have stated, it is acceptable. Regarding the treatment of atrophy, why do we specifically focus on Yangming?" Qibo replied: "Yangming is the sea of the five organs and six bowels, responsible for nourishing the primary tendons, which bind the bones and facilitate movement. The Chong vessel is the sea of the meridians, responsible for moistening and irrigating the valleys. It connects with Yangming at the primary tendons. The convergence of Yin and Yang is in the primary tendons, meeting at the Qi street, with Yangming being the primary force there, all belonging to the belt vessel and linked to the governing vessel. Therefore, if Yangming is deficient, the primary tendons become lax, and the belt vessel does not constrict, resulting in the legs becoming atrophied and unable to function.

    [Note: The five organs and six bowels all receive Qi from Yangming, hence it is called the sea of the organs and bowels. The front Yin is where the primary tendons gather; Taiyin and Yangming converge there, forming the meeting point of all tendons. The blood and Qi generated by Yangming nourish this gathering, which is why we specifically treat atrophies with Yangming. The Chong vessel arises in the womb and ascends along the back, acting as the sea of meridians. Its superficial aspects irrigate the valleys and converge with Yangming at the primary tendons. Lesser Yin, Taiyin, and Yangming come together in the Chong, Ren, and Du vessels, all converging at the primary tendons, ascending through the abdomen and returning to the Qi street, thus Yangming governs this, making it the dominant force.][9p]
    The three Yin and three Yang, along with the twelve main channels and the extraordinary vessels Ren, Du, and Chong, follow a path through the upper and lower parts of the body, all constrained by the girdle vessel. The Du vessel begins at the perineum, bifurcating into Ren and Chong as it ascends along the back and abdomen; thus, Ren, Chong, Shaoyin, and Yangming are all interconnected. Yangming is the sea of water and grain, responsible for nourishing the tendons and ligaments. If deficient, the tendons and ligaments loosen, failing to support the bones and facilitating dysfunction, leading to atrophy. If the girdle vessel cannot extend, the tendons in the lower body loosen, causing the feet to weaken and be unusable; thus, these issues of atrophy stem from Yangming.

    The Emperor asked, "How should this be treated?" Qi Bo replied, "Nourish their essence and unblock their acupoints, balance their deficiency and excess, harmonize their ascending and descending flow. Tendons, channels, bones, and muscles receive their influences according to the seasons; then the illness will be resolved." The Emperor said, "Good!"

    (Note: Nourishing their essence refers to replenishing the true Qi of the five organs. Unblocking refers to facilitating the heat of the five organs. Balancing deficiency and excess means replenishing Qi when deficient and draining when heat is excessive. Harmonizing ascending and descending refers to regulating the flow of Qi. Tendons, channels, bones, and muscles correspond with the five organs, whose Qi responds externally to the four seasons, each receiving the seasonal Qi at the appropriate time to correspond with their depth; then the illness will be resolved.)[10p]
    Pulse Diagnosis Chapter

    If the illness is mildly deficient, it leads to lameness; during the first month, the Yang energy thaws the ground energy and emerges. The so-called mild deficiency arises from significant insufficiency during the winter cold, thus it results in lameness.

    Qi Interaction and Transformation Discussion

    When the soil is excessive in the year, rain is abundant and flowing, the kidney water is affected by pathogens, causing ailments such as atrophy and discomfort in the feet.

    When the fire is insufficient in the year, cold prevails widely, resulting in diseases like weakness and paralysis, where the feet cannot support the body.

    Five Constant Governance Discussion

    When the Yangming governs the heavens, the tendons suffer atrophy, unable to stand for long.[11p]
    The Great Discussion on the Six Primary Yangs

    The Sun governs the heavenly order. When people suffer from cold and dampness, their muscles wither, and their legs are weak and unable to move. [Note: This is due to the affliction of cold and dampness.] The Sun governs the heavenly order; when the four elements are present, the muscles wither, and the legs become weak. [Note: The withering of muscles and weakness of legs is due to the dampness of the earth.]

    The Essential Discussion of Supreme Truth

    When the Taiyin is in the spring, excess leads to weakness in the legs and a heavy lower body. [Note: The heaviness and weakness in the legs result from dampness in the spring, which invades the Taiyin meridian.] All forms of weakness, panting, and vomiting are associated with the upper body. [Note: They ascend from below to above.]

    The Spiritual Pivot Classic

    The Section on the Manifestations of Diseases in the Organs Due to Pathogenic Factors

    The spleen pulse being very slack indicates weakness and numbness; mildly slack indicates wind-induced weakness, where the limbs are unusable, and the heart feels clear as if there were no disease. [Note: Weakness and wind-induced weakness both result in the paralysis of the limbs; in severe cases, the disease manifests externally due to internal issues, while in mild cases, the affliction is external without affecting the internal organs, leading the heart to feel clear of disease.] The lung pulse being slightly slack indicates weakness and pathogenic leakage, with sweating below the head that cannot be stopped. [Note: Slackness indicates severe heat causing excessive sweating; lung heat affects the muscles, leading to weakness.] The kidney pulse being slightly slippery indicates bone weakness, where one cannot rise from sitting, and when one stands, vision is impaired. [Note: Bone weakness prevents standing due to heat harming kidney energy; impaired vision results from heat damaging the essence of the bones.][12p]
    Article on the Spirit

    Fear without understanding injures the essence; when the essence is injured, the bones become sore, weak, and lead to cold limbs. The essence naturally depletes from below.

    Article on the Meridians

    The foot Shaoyin channel governs the diseases of the kidney. Symptoms include weakness, cold limbs, a strong desire to lie down, heat and pain in the lower limbs. When pronounced, the pulse at the wrist is twice the size of that at the vessel's entrance; when weak, the wrist pulse is smaller than that at the vessel's entrance.

    Article on Yin and Yang (Twenty-Five Types)

    When the foot Yangming is deficient in blood and rich in qi, the flesh becomes dense, leading to weakness in the limbs and numbness in the feet. This is because insufficient blood and qi cannot nourish the tendons and bones.

    Essentials of the Golden Cabinet (by Zhang Ji)

    Bone Atrophy

    Sour flavors injure the tendons; when the tendons are injured, they weaken, which is termed "leakage." Salty flavors injure the bones; when the bones are injured, they atrophy, which is termed "withered." When leakage and withering clash, it is termed "disrupted leakage." When the nourishing qi does not flow, and the protective qi cannot act alone, both are weak, the three burners cannot regulate, the four modalities are interrupted, the body becomes emaciated, one foot is swollen, yellow sweat appears, and the limbs are cold. If fever occurs, it indicates a rheumatic condition.[13p]
    On Febrile Diseases by Zhang Jie

    After vomiting and purging from febrile disease, one may experience voiding symptoms of restlessness, with a very weak pulse. If after eight or nine days one has a feeling of fullness and hardness under the heart, pain beneath the ribs, and a sensation of qi rushing upwards to the throat, dizziness, and restlessness in the meridians, this can lead to atrophy over time.

    On the Matters of Confucian Filial Piety by Zhang Congzheng

    The four conditions of wind, bi syndrome, atrophy, and syncope are described in detail in the Inner Canon. To elaborate further may seem redundant, but it is not. This is for those in modern times who do not read the Inner Canon and misinterpret its essential meanings. The four conditions of wind, bi, atrophy, and syncope are fundamentally different, yet modern practitioners fail to distinguish them, treating them all as cold due to wind and addressing the deficiency with tonics. This misunderstanding leads to prolonged suffering without resolution. The diseases of the extremities manifest differently: the vigorous ones indicate wind, the numb or painful suggest bi syndrome, weakness and lack of use correspond to atrophy, and the cold or heat that inversely occur indicate syncope. Their root causes are also vastly different. Wind is often a combination of wind and heat; bi syndrome involves wind, dampness, and cold; atrophy arises from fire overcoming metal; and syncope can originate from either cold or heat, both arising from below. Current practitioners overlook these origins and mistakenly assign the label of wind upon seeing hands and feet drooping. Yet, as stated in the Zuo Zhuan, wind can lead to myriad diseases of the extremities. Are we not aware that the six atmospheric factors of heat, dryness, dampness, fire, and cold can all cause these four extremity illnesses? I dare to elaborate further in the Zuo Zhuan for those eager to save lives; one may choose wisely from it.[14p]
    The condition of weakness, known as "wei," manifests as feeble legs that can hardly walk. This is due to the kidney essence (water) being unable to dominate the heart fire. The heart fire rises to disturb the lung metal, which in turn, is restrained by this fire. The six branches (corresponding to the six organs) then become scorched, leading to a weakness of the skin and hair, resulting in the development of wei.

    "Zha" (or “leg weakness”) refers to an inability of the legs to stretch or move properly. The kidney is considered the offspring of the lung's metal. When the kidney water is diminished, it allows the fire to rise uncontrollably. The kidney governs the marrow, and if the fire is excessive, it depletes the marrow, which leads to an internal excess causing this condition.

    According to the "Essential Treatise on the True and Great," both wei and conditions of breathlessness and vomiting pertain to the upper region (upper jiao). The three jiaos represent the hand's lesser yang and fire. The ailments of wei, breathlessness, and vomiting all exist above the diaphragm and are associated with the lung metal. Thus, muscle weakness leading to pulse weakness is termed "pulse wei," dampness leading to numbness is termed "flesh wei," marrow depletion causing leg weakness is termed "bone wei," and excess in the chamber leads to tendon weakness.

    Overall, the causes of wei as a disease are due to excess heat affecting the body, indulgence in desires, excessive force, gradually leading to the condition of wei. Therefore, wei in various forms is associated with the lung, pulse wei with the heart, tendon wei with the liver, flesh wei with the spleen, and bone wei with the kidney. The underlying reason is that the lung is affected by heat, causing its leaves to wither, transmitting this heat to the four organs, resulting in the development of the disease. It is clearly stated that wei conditions do not arise from cold.

    Thus, the occurrence of wei is particularly notable in the months of May, June, and July. "Wu" (midday) is designated as the position of the lesser yin fire, "wei" (the earth element) is where dampness, metal, and fire intertwine, and "shen" (the evening) relates to the lesser yang fire. Hence, wei arises during these three months due to heat. Consequently, those suffering from wei will have a pulse that is superficial and large.[15p]
    Nowadays, those who use medicine often see weakness in the knees and legs, difficulty in walking, or one foot unable to extend, and treat it as cold and damp foot bi-syndrome. They hastily use herbs like black, aconite, milk, myrrh, natural copper, and king's herb, applying fire from moxa needles, boiling, and steaming with pouches. The weakness worsens, and they die—could this be fate? The treatment for weakness and blockage differs greatly. Wind, cold, and damp blockage can still be treated with steaming, boiling, and moxibustion, and may sometimes yield results. However, using such methods for weakness only aggravates the condition—why is that? Weakness stems from heat in the lungs, leading to a 'burnt leaf' condition, which transmits to the five organs; could there be any cold? Treating weakness as a cold condition is akin to killing without a blade. Weakness does not lead to death; deaths arise from erroneous medication.

    Once, a soldier named Song Ziyu stationed in Xi City, unexpectedly developed weakness during May and June, unable to use his waist and legs, becoming bedridden. He sought my treatment. Examining his hands, both pulses were slippery and rapid yet strong. I relied on the "Inner Canon" principles that fire indulgence leads to internal heat while causing coldness around the diaphragm due to phlegm. Recent conditions signify heat, while old ones signify cold. Food and drink that linger in the upper abdomen might also be addressed. After the phlegm was resolved, I administered downward treatments, a few dozen times, and he gradually regained clarity in thought and improved appetite. His legs and feet began to lift, his knees straightened, with the heart descending and the kidneys rising. I then followed with a detoxifying decoction of Coptis, adding Angelica and other herbs, along with Heart-Soothing Decoction, Cooling the Diaphragm Powder, and Chaihu Decoction, taking them regularly.[16p]
    The classics say: To treat diseases of the heart and lungs, the methods are closest; therefore, medications should be used frequently but in small doses. To treat diseases of the kidneys and liver, the methods are furthest; hence, medications may be used less frequently but in larger quantities. Many people find this surprising, yet when I treat atrophy, I commonly apply these methods as if picking up lost items. If I were to deceive others with this, would it not be akin to incurring divine wrath? This is the evidence of Song Ziyu, thus it must be recorded and shown as a testament for future generations. Therefore, the "Inner Classic" states that the method for treating atrophy uniquely utilizes the Yangming channel, which is the stomach vessel, the sea of the five organs and six bowels, responsible for nourishing the foundational tendons. The foundational tendons govern the binding of bones, which are located below the navel and above the pubic hair. Additionally, it governs great bodily movements; these movements are the major joints of the body that regulate bending and stretching. Thus, if Yangming is deficient, the foundational tendons become lax; if the foundational tendons are lax, the major vessels do not extend, resulting in weakness and atrophy of the legs.[17p]
    When one takes Yangming, it refers to the stomach channel. The stomach is the sea of food and water, the center of human wellbeing in all seasons. With the qi of the stomach as the foundation, if the foundation is strong, then essence can transform; when essence transforms, marrow is nourished; and when marrow is nourished, one can move freely. According to the theory of Yin and Yang correspondence: if one’s form is inadequate, it should be warmed with qi; if essence is lacking, it should be replenished with flavor. Flavor refers to the five tastes; when the five tastes are harmonized, they can replenish essence and enhance qi. The five tastes come from five grains, five vegetables, five fruits, and five kinds of meat. The five tastes value harmony and should not overly favor one. Moreover, it is said: tranquility and emptiness lead to the true qi, and the spirit is internally contained; how can illness arise? If one supplements with metals, stones, grasses, and woods, it will surely increase qi over time. The natural course of transformation results in increased qi over a long duration, leading to premature decline. Thus, people who consume yellow root and苦参 for a long time can turn hot, and those who long consume hot medicines may not end up cold-hearted![18p]
    On the Effects of Damp Heat Leading to Atrophy of the Lungs and the Reception of Pathogens

    Between June and July, dampness prevails, allowing the child to generate abundant heat for the mother. The combination of damp and heat injures the metal (lungs) and severely affects the large intestine, thus cold and cool treatments are required to counteract it. The dry metal succumbs to the damp heat pathogen, severing the source of cold water that supports transformation. When the source is cut off, the kidneys become deficient, leading to severe cases of atrophy and paralysis—resulting in weakness and inability to move from the waist down, and lack of balance in walking, with both feet leaning sideways. Clear and drying decoction should be the remedy.

    On Heat Injuries to Gastric Qi

    The "Needling Intent" states: “When Qi is deficient and the body is hot, it results from heat injury.” The "Atrophy Treatise" mentions: “After long journeys and exertion, encountering excessive heat and thirst, the yang qi suffers internally; when internally attacked, the heat retreats to the kidneys, which are the body's water organ. Now if water cannot overcome fire, the bones wither and the marrow becomes deficient, leading to limb weakness and resulting in bone atrophy.” Thus, the "Lower Classic" states: “Bone atrophy arises from excessive heat.” This damp heat leads to atrophy, causing weakness and lack of strength in the bones, therefore treatment for atrophy must focus on the Yangming channel.

    During the long summer, when damp heat prevails and is intensely strong, many people feel their limbs burdened, spirits low, and sluggishness to movement. Symptoms include fullness in the chest, shortness of breath, heaviness and pain in the joints, sometimes with elevated Qi causing wheezing, body heat and restlessness; distension in the heart region, urine that is yellow and scant, loose stools occurring frequently, possibly with diarrhea of yellowish mucus or resembling rice water, with variations in thirst, disinterest in food, sweating, a heavy body, or diminished sweating—often blood diseases appear prior to Qi deficiency.[19p]
    In the pulse, if one feels a strong and slow rhythm, it indicates that dampness is clashing with internal factors, leading to a further delay. Though the symptoms may vary, the heat and dampness from the season remain the same. It is advisable to treat this with a clearing and drying formula, called the Qing Shu Yi Qi Decoction.

    If the damp heat affects the kidneys and liver, causing improper movement and weakness in the knees and feet, resulting in a misalignment of the legs, this indicates the presence of atrophy pathology. Add five dashes of wine-fried Huangbo and Zhimu to help restore vitality to the legs.

    Atrophy occurs due to damp heat attacking the kidneys and liver, and it must be urgently addressed. Otherwise, the underlying energy in the lower body may be entirely exhausted, leading to paralysis where one cannot move the lower body, accompanied by persistent restlessness and anxiety.

    If there is a sensation of heaviness, decreased body weight, normal respiration, and regular urination, and once the damp heat has retreated, or if the added pathogens diminish, there is no need to use the herbs like Wuweizi, Zexie, Zhuling, Fuling, Huangbo, Zhimu, Cangzhu, or Baizhu. Instead, rely on adjusting the medications based on the original symptoms and maintain regular intake of prescribed herbs. It is also essential to incorporate two to three dashes of wine-fried Huangbo.[20p]
    Danxi's Heart Method [Yuan - Zhu Zhenheng]

    Treatment of Atrophy Syndrome

    The atrophy syndrome must not be treated with wind methods or wind medicines.
    Atrophy syndrome includes damp-heat, damp-phlegm, qi deficiency, blood deficiency, and stagnant blood. For damp-heat, use Dongyuan Jianbu Pill, to dry dampness and reduce yin fire, along with herbs like Atractylodes, Scutellaria, Huangbo, and Achyranthes. For damp-phlegm, use Erchen Decoction with added Atractylodes, Bai Zhu, Scutellaria, Huangbo, Bamboo Juice, and Ginger Juice. For qi deficiency, use Four Gentlemen Decoction with added Scutellaria, Huangbo, and Atractylodes. For blood deficiency, use Four Substance Decoction with added Huangbo and Atractylodes, decoct, and take with Yin Nourishing Pill. There might also be food stagnation and dead blood obstructing downward movement, generally belonging to heat; use Shen Zhu Siwu Decoction, Huangbo, and similar remedies.

    Regarding the five forms of atrophy and related syndromes, a special chapter was dedicated, discussing it in detail; later prescriptions often lack focus on this syndrome. The reason stems from confusion with wind stroke categories. Master Danxi laments the longstanding issues, empathizing with those suffering from this ailment, as many misled by incompetent doctors. Those committed to this field must deeply understand it. Indeed, Chen Wuzhe stated that atrophy results from insufficient internal organs, which is quite valid. However, what is insufficient in atrophy is primarily yin blood, yet all these prescriptions mainly aim to tonify yang and replenish qi—how can one avoid the issues of deficiency and excess? Furthermore, Wuzhe’s principles of the three causes in formulating prescriptions could be considered the best amongst all; however, even he was not clear on this syndrome, let alone others.[21p]
    Medical Overview
    Compiled by Lou Ying (Ming Dynasty)

    General Discussion

    Someone asked: "In treating atrophy, one only considers the Yangming channel; this seems to suggest a lack of expansion in understanding. Please clarify for me." I answered: "All forms of atrophy arise from heat in the lungs. Just this one phrase conveys the essence of the treatment method. The classics state: 'The east is real while the west is empty; drain the south and nourish the north.' This indeed refers to the concepts of generation and overcoming, and the grand principles are not beyond this. The east corresponds to wood, which is the liver. The west corresponds to metal, which is the lungs. The south corresponds to fire, which is the heart. The north corresponds to water, which is the kidneys. Among the five directions, only fire has dual aspects. Though the kidneys have two, water resides in one of them. Yang is always in excess, while yin is perpetually insufficient. Hence the texts state, 'One water cannot overcome two fires,' which is a necessary principle. The nature of metal is dry and resides above, governing qi, and it fears fire. The nature of earth is warm and resides in the center, governing the limbs, and it fears wood. The nature of fire is to ascend; if desires become excessive, then water loses its ability to nourish, and fire, in its fear, becomes diminished and falls prey to what overcomes it. When the lungs are afflicted by fire, they become heated. Wood, having a rigid and abrupt nature, when the lungs are overheated, loses its ability to nourish; similarly, when the spleen is afflicted by wood, it suffers damage. If the lungs are hot, they cannot regulate the entire body. If the spleen is damaged, the limbs cannot function, resulting in all forms of atrophy.[22p]
    If the south is purged, then the lung's metal is clear, but the east remains imbalanced; what harm is there to the spleen? If the north is supplemented, then the heart's fire diminishes and the west is not empty; what heat is there in the lungs? Thus, when the Yangming is full, the tendons are nourished, capable of binding the bones and benefiting the mechanisms of the body. The methods for treating atrophy do not exceed this. Luo Longji also said: when wind and fire are intense, one should nourish the kidney water. Dr. Dongyuan takes ephedra as the sovereign, with astragalus and other tonics assisting, to treat various forms of atrophy without a fixed method. There are cases with phlegm accumulation, excess dampness, excess heat, a mixture of dampness and heat, and those with Qi entrapment; when faced with illness, the prescription is made based on the symptoms. Those skilled in treating atrophy can manage it well! Nonetheless, if the principles are misapplied, treatment may fail. Heaven produces Yang, thickening the Qi and generating heat; ancient sages stated that for those suffering from atrophy, if they do not restrain their tastes, I know they will not find peace.

    When heart heat is severe, the fire uniquely blazes upwards. The kidney's pulse usually descends, but when fire is intense and ascends, the kidney pulse also rises in accordance with the blazing fire. The Yin Qi rebels; fire inside burns higher; the Yin rises separating from the Yang, failing to maintain its position below. The heart's Qi flows through the vessels, thus yielding atrophy, the knees and wrists resembling those that have been broken, unable to assist one another; the meridians and tendons slacken and do not support the body. One may descend for hundreds of lines and still recover.[23p]
    Differentiation of the Treatments for Atrophy Disease and Wind Disease

    Based on discussions in Danxi's writings, atrophy disease and wind disease are treated differently. This is because of the treatment for stroke mentioned in the "Jufang," where Menglang uses diaphoretic and damp-expelling medicines that harm blood and qi, attacking the root cause, without understanding methods for nourishment. Thus, he references atrophy disease to remedy the shortcomings of the "Jufang," and his words reflect this perspective. However, the symptoms outlined in the "Jufang" for stroke—such as uncoordinated limbs, needing assistance to stand, mental confusion, and incoherent speech—are indeed similar to those of heat disease as described in the "Neijing." It is essential to note the differences. The "Zhenjing" states that when genuine qi departs and the pathogenic factor remains, it leads to partial atrophy. The discourse on atrophy indicates that when the Yangming is deficient, the muscles relax, the belt vessel fails to draw, leading to atrophy and inability to use the feet. Thus, it is understood that the uncoordinated limbs in the case of atrophy occur due to obstructing evil qi in the channels, whereas in the case of atrophy disease, it is due to a deficiency in Yangming, muscle relaxation, and failing belt vessel. There are symptoms of erratic will in atrophy disease, but none in true atrophy disease itself. The disease of atrophy is also known as wind atrophy, combining internal injury and external invasion, while atrophy disease is solely induced by internal injury. Wind atrophy occurs due to violent impacts, while atrophy disease develops gradually due to laziness. All of this clarifies that atrophy and atrophy disease are distinct ailments.

    From "Miscellaneous Writings of Ming Physicians" by Wang Lun, Ming Dynasty.

    Atrophy syndrome should not be treated as wind disease.[24p]
    Paralysis and weakness are diseases caused by the absence of blood and the combination of phlegm, fire, and damp heat. The ancients said that one should not treat these with wind-dispelling methods, yet they often used wind herbs like Xiao Xu Ming Decoction, West Zhou Xu Ming Decoction, and dispelling wind decoction, which contain ingredients such as Qiang Huo, Fang Feng, Ma Huang, Gui Zhi, Wu Tou, and Xi Xin. These herbs disperse wind evils and open the pores. However, improper use may further dry up the yin and blood.

    Note: According to previous observations, many cases in Jiangnan are attributed to yin deficiency and qi deficiency, with damp heat affecting the fire. Paralysis and weakness often pertain to the yin deficiency and damp heat in the Yangming channels of the hands and feet, so the diagnosis must be made with care.

    Medical Classics Must Read 【Ming Dynasty, Li Zhongzi】

    General Discussion

    It is noted that weakness is a serious illness, hence the "Inner Classic" repeatedly discusses it. Earlier scholars varied in their compilations on this topic, sometimes associating it with deficiency and labor, or with wind and dampness, which strays from the core principles. Only Dan Xi particularly emphasized its significance, though his explanations were somewhat lacking. The classic discusses how the root of diseases involves all five organs but especially emphasizes the Taiyin Lung channel. It also states that while treatment methods involve various channels, special emphasis is placed on the Yangming Stomach channel.

    Why this emphasis? The lung, being of the metal element, is dry in nature and is located above, governing the transformation of qi to circulate throughout the body, being sensitive to fire. When the heat and fire of the five organs vaporize, the metal element is weakened, leading to lung heat and damage. Thus, the diseases vary among the five organs but the place of the hand Taiyin is commonly affected. The spleen, being of the earth element, is damp and located centrally, receiving grains and water to nourish the limbs, also being sensitive to the wood element.[25p]
    When the lung meridian is invaded and loses its proper state, the wood is not restrained and insults what it conquers. Therefore, the treatment principles are categorized by the five organs, and no part of the foot yangming is overlooked. Since it is said that the lung is harmed, treatment should focus on the lung. But why does Qibo exclusively select yangming? According to the "Ling Shu," the true qi received from heaven combines with the grain qi to fill the body. If yangming is deficient, the five organs cannot receive their nourishment and cannot circulate blood and qi, nourish the sinews and bones, or benefit the joints. Therefore, when the body is unable to receive food and water, it results in atrophy; it is not merely about selecting yangming. This is why Dancai says: draining the south clears the lung metal, and the east becomes deficient, so how can the stomach be harmed? Enriching the north causes the heart fire to descend and the west to remain unexhausted, so how can there be heat in the lungs? This statement is indeed correct. If the stomach is deficient and appetite decreases, it should be treated with aromatic and warm medications. If one is overly concerned with the idea of draining the south, the stomach will worsen. If one can apply treatment based on this principle, half the battle against atrophy will have been won. As for the seven emotions and six excesses, which have many variations, formulating prescriptions based on specific illnesses cannot be fully expressed through words.[26p]
    Treatment Methods

    For excess heart fire, use Liangge San. For liver fire disturbance, use Xiao Chai Hu Tang. For water deficiency and fire inflammation, use Liu Wei Di Huang Wan. For excessive phlegm, use Bei Mu Lou San. When heart qi is hot leading to weak pulse, use iron powder, silver foil, Coptis, bitter root, Gentian, honey stone, bull's gall, dragon’s teeth, gentian, white lichen, peony bark, earth bone bark, thunderbolts, and rhinoceros horn. When liver qi is hot leading to weak muscles, use raw Rehmannia, Asparagus, lily, purple vine, white thistle, Eucommia, rhizome of Dioscorea, dodder seed, Chinese achyranthes, windbreak herb, Scutellaria, and Coptis. When spleen qi is hot leading to weak flesh, use two types of Atractylodes, two types of Chen Pi, and Xiatian Gao. When kidney qi is hot leading to weak bones, use Jingang Wan, Niu Xi Wan, Jiawei Sijun Wan, and nourishing kidney pills. For lung heat leading to weakness, use Astragalus, Asparagus, Dendrobium, lily, yam, rhinoceros horn, tonca wood, Platycodon, dry Scutellaria, gardenia, apricot kernel, and gentian. For combined damp-heat, use Jianbu Wan plus Huangbo, Atractylodes, Scutellaria, or Qingzao Tang. For damp phlegm, use two types of Chen Pi, two types of Miao, bamboo juice, and ginger juice.[27p]
    Blood Deficiency: Si Wu Decoction, Er Miao Powder, Bu Yin Pill.
    Qi Deficiency: Si Jun Zi Decoction combined with Er Miao Powder.
    Both Qi and Blood Deficiency: Shi Quan Da Bu Decoction.
    Food Stagnation: Mu Xiang Bing Lang Pill.
    Stagnant Blood: Tao Ren, Hong Hua, Peng Zhu, Chuan Shan Jia, Si Wu Decoction.
    Excess Stagnation: San Hua Decoction, Cheng Qi Decoction; purging several times results in recovery.
    Deficiency of the Kidney and Liver: Bu Yi Shen Gan Pill, Shen Gui Zi Yin Pill, Bu Yi Pill, Hu Qian Pill.

    General Principles in Diagnosis and Treatment
    (Ming Dynasty, Wang Keng Tang)

    General Discussion

    Weakness: Refers to limbs that are soft and powerless, with joints relaxed and not contracting. The sage deems weakness to be crucial among various ailments, thus dedicating a section to it. It divides the heat of the five organs into named diseases, their corresponding skin, vessels, tendons, muscles, and bones leading to the feet being unable to support weight. It narrates the evils of heat affecting the five organs, indicating that each organ is harmed by a specific evil. The subtle meaning here is that through the five emotions, five labors, and six excesses, one may affirm the desires of the organ qi, each providing an example. If one comprehends the eighty-one sections, one can see that the five labors, five emotions, and six excesses indeed produce the heat of the five organs resulting in weakness.[28p]
    What is it that causes this? It is said that when lung qi is hot, the skin becomes withered, resulting from loss and unfulfilled desires; similar to another text which states that one who is initially rich may later become poor. Although it may not harm the external influences, the skin burns and tendons contract, leading to weakness and paralysis. This is linked to the seven emotions being suppressed. If we discuss conditions like atrophy, shortness of breath, nausea, or feelings of anger and depression, all belong to the aforementioned. Therefore, how can we say that any heat does not cause atrophy, or that any pulse is unaffected by heat? For instance, if heart qi is hot, it leads to pulse atrophy, as this arises from excessive sorrow, where yang energy moves inward, causing blood to collapse and the pulse to become vacuous, leading to pulse atrophy. This regards the heart as the governing organ of spirit, essential to the pulse. Furthermore, each of the five organs has its spirit and aspirations. When anger arises, it causes qi to ascend abnormally, and in severe cases, it may lead to vomiting blood—manifestations of the five emotions. Heat injuring blood causes blood to reverse in the meridians, leading to emptiness, as if joy in form meets sorrow in aspiration. When ailments arise in the pulse, all five emotions contribute to its atrophy, not solely sorrow. Additionally, the five emotions affecting each organ causes damage to their associated spirits. Severe anger can force tendons to stretch, while joy manifests in bodily illness, resulting in joy in spirit and suffering in form, whereas joy in form and sorrow in aspiration leads to illness in the tendons.[29p]
    If one is troubled by anxiety, fear, joy, or anger, then due to the vast void, the five organs affect each other, leading to illnesses manifesting in twenty-five variations. This results in conditions such as the decay of the large bones and the sinking of the flesh. The spirit and will within the five organs can be considered as heat illnesses, which cannot be enumerated. For instance, if the liver's energy is heated due to endless thoughts, and desires remain unfulfilled, it is akin to the lungs' unfulfilled longing. Excessive indulgence in sexual relations causes relaxation of the fundamental tendons, similar to the exhaustion resulting from long travels of the kidneys. From this, it is evident that the five kinds of labor can each cause harm to the corresponding skin, flesh, tendons, and bones of the five organs. For example, if the spleen's energy is heated, it is influenced gradually by dampness, which is related to matters involving water. If, in a given year, the Yin earth is damp, with the heavens controlling moisture from springs, this leads to muscle atrophy and the feet becoming weak and unable to recover. This serves as an example of how external factors arise from the five organs. The same applies to all other organs.[30p]
    The return of lesser yin leads to muscle atrophy; the return of lesser yang leads to pulse atrophy; the governance of yangming over heaven’s affairs results in bone atrophy influenced by the four energies. The governance of jueyin confirms that wind energy descends, and spleen energy ascends, resulting in atrophy of the muscles. There are conditions due to dampness, initially felt as wrapping; if damp heat persists and is not dispelled, the large muscles become soft and short, while the small muscles are loose and elongated, where softness and shortness denote rigidity, and looseness and elongation denote atrophy. The "Ling Shu" discusses the eight transformations of wind, which can injure muscles, flesh, or bones, with excess heat causing the muscles to loosen, bones to wither, and flesh to decay. Thus, it is common for external excess to give rise to diseases of the five viscera.

    However, some do not mention the external pathogens, but instead discuss the conditions arising from internal deficiencies of organs. For example, a spleen ailment denotes a heavy body and muscle atrophy, with foot atrophy resulting in difficulty walking; kidney deficiency can lead to lameness or paralysis; a surplus of the three yangs with insufficient three yins leads to unevenness; if the small yang of the foot is deficient, it results in atrophy of the legs, preventing one from rising from a seated position; if the small yang of the foot is deficient, one may experience failure to retract the feet and thin shins. Furthermore, injuries from diet occur; an overly salty flavor can fatigue the bone energy, and an overly spicy flavor can weaken the tendons and meridians, particularly among those indulging in rich foods, leading to uneven atrophy and fatigue.[31p]
    What has been presented above pertains solely to the condition of weakness due to insufficient use of the original principles. As for the case when all five organs are intensely heated, causing the spirit to become dim and collapse, and both hands and feet to lose function—what is commonly referred to as paralysis—surely this too is a severe form of weakness. Furthermore, in the subsequent discussion of lung weakness as an organ disease, there are also cases where heart qi weakness results in death, indicating that all five organs can indeed be afflicted by weakness; this can be understood. The "Original Disease Theory" discusses the involuntary loss of urine, stating that lung heat afflicts the kidney area, impeding the circulation in the foot's Shao Yin channel, so that qi and blood cannot flow freely, leading to weakness and paralysis. This causes the spirit to be ineffective and unable to perform its functions, resulting in the involuntary loss of urine and feces. Therefore, from this discussion, it is evident that whenever the spirit, qi, or blood is either weak or obstructed, any of the organs, channels, or related areas—internally or externally—may become dysfunctional. The "Inner Classic" emphasizes this matter, repeatedly addressing it in various chapters, yet later readers have completely missed its essence.[32p]
    The gathering of discussions on theories often sees either the concept of "excessive labor" or the "Wind Gate." It was Danxi who first elucidated errors that had persisted for over a thousand years, and yet his explanations remain unclear; this is regrettable. It is stated: "The discussion of atrophy involves the Yangming channel, aligning with the ancestral sinews at the Qi Street. Due to Yangming deficiency, the ancestral sinews become lax, and the belt channel fails to pull, resulting in foot atrophy, hence the sole focus on Yangming." Now, how shall you treat the various conditions of illness and atrophy that you have examined? It is said: "The sage speaks of one principle, which can be extrapolated to ten or a hundred; is it solely about foot atrophy? The principle of complementing each channel and assisting its roots, understanding their deficiencies and strengths, harmonizing the counterflow and proper flow, contains all treatment methods for dispelling pathogens." The mention of regulating the channels, muscles, and flesh, according to their seasonal afflictions, also pertains to the treatment methods for the various conditions of atrophy. Nonetheless, it is noted that no case of atrophy arises without the deficiency of Yangming.[33p]
    How can this be explained? According to the "Ling Shu," it is said that the true qi is received from Heaven and combines with the food qi to fill the body. It is also stated that food first enters the stomach's two regions to nourish the five organs and separately branches out into the two pathways of nourishment and defense. The great qi that stagnates and does not flow accumulates in the chest, known as the sea of qi. The "Su Wen" states that the foot Taiyin is the three Yin; its channels traverse the stomach and belong to the spleen, thus Taiyin circulates qi within the three Yin. The Yangming represents the exterior and serves as the sea of the five organs and six bowels, circulating qi in the three Yang. Each organ receives qi from Yangming via its respective channels, hence the stomach facilitates the movement of its fluids. When the limbs are unable to absorb the qi of food and water, they gradually weaken day by day, leading to blockages in the Yin pathways; the tendons, bones, and muscles lack the qi to thrive, thus they do not function properly. The Chong vessel originates below the kidneys and connects with the Ren vessel that arises from the womb, governing the blood sea; it is also referred to as the sea of the five organs and six bowels, all of which derive nourishment from it.[34p]
    The upper channels absorb the three Yang and nourish all essence, while the lower channels absorb the three Yin and nourish all sinews, warming the muscles. They connect with the Yongming channel at the Qi Chong point. Hence, when Yongming is deficient, the channel is loose, and the Dai Mai is unresponsive, resulting in foot weakness and inability to move. Observing this, we see that true Qi is the way of heaven, while grain Qi is the way of earth. Without heaven, earth cannot generate; without earth, heaven cannot be realized. Therefore, true Qi and grain Qi must come together to create life, establishing the pathway for physical Qi. Thus, when Yongming is deficient and the five organs receive nothing, they cannot circulate the blood and Qi, nourish Yin and Yang, moisten the sinews and bones, or facilitate joints. If the Qi Sea receives nothing, then the Wei Qi cannot warm and nourish the flesh, fill the skin, or support the opening and closing of the pores. If the Blood Sea receives nothing, then the channels outside and inside are empty; consequently, the spirit, Qi, and blood that sustain life become weak. Therefore, in the entire body, wherever it cannot receive the water and grain Qi, it becomes nonfunctional and results in atrophy. To treat atrophy is not merely to address Yongming; what else could be done?[35p]
    Discussion on Evidence

    The meaning of atrophy, as detailed in the "Internal Classic," indicates that the symptoms associated with the five organs are all related to heat. These symptoms arise from the heat of the lungs, leading to dryness in the metal element and depletion of the water element, thus resulting in atrophy. The treatment theories of Danxi indeed align with this. However, upon closer examination of the texts, it is stated that excessive sorrow can obstruct the collaterals, leading to pulse atrophy. Endless thoughts and unmet desires can cause muscle atrophy, while dampness can cause water-related issues, leading to atrophy of flesh; therefore, they are not entirely due to heat. This suggests a complexity—there are those who generate heat from such conditions, but there are also those whose vital energy is harmed. When vital energy is damaged, it can result in insufficient essence to nourish, and insufficient blood to sustain; this is quite common as well.[36p]
    If one were to generally discuss fire, there would be a concern that genuine yang might be depleted, and that soil might weaken and water dry up, which some could not endure. Therefore, one should consider the shallow and deep aspects of cold and heat, carefully examine the voids and fullness, as well as the urgency of conditions in order to administer treatment, so that a complete cure for atrophy might be achieved.

    The classics state: "If dampness and heat are not eliminated, the large tendons become soft and short, while the small tendons become slack and long; softness and shortness lead to rigidity, while slackness and length lead to atrophy." This pronouncement from the Inner Canon on tendon diseases is merely an illustrative remark meant to inspire self-reflection. It does not imply that the large tendons must be without slackness or that the small tendons must be free from softness and shortness. For instance, atrophy and weakness surely derive from slackness and length; could the large tendons truly be unaffected? The meaning conveyed in this classic can be understood from this perspective. Thus, beyond the condition of atrophy, whenever encountering diseases like spasm, one must recognize that rigidity arises from softness and shortness, while paralysis and weakness result from slackness and length. This captures the essence of the Inner Canon, and one can also obtain the rationale for distinguishing between cold, heat, dryness, and dampness.[37p]
    On Treatment

    Generally, weakness caused by damp heat presents with a rapid and slippery pulse and many signs of restlessness and heat, so one must first eliminate the heat. The Er Miao San (Two Wonderful Powder) is appropriate, adjusting according to the symptoms. If there is both yin deficiency and heat, one should use a modified version of the Si Wu Tang (Four Substance Decoction), Hu Jing Gu Wan (Tiger Bone Pill), or similar formulas like Dan Xi Bu Yin Wan (Dan Xi's Yin Supplement Pill) and Zi Yin Ba Wei Wan (Eight Flavor Nourishing Pills). If there is absolutely no heat present and the condition arises solely from kidney water deficiency and liver blood deficiency, one should avoid using cooling medicines that could weaken the vital energy. The best option is Lu Jiao Jiao Wan (Deer Horn Gel Pill) or formulas like Jia Wei Si Jin Wan (Modified Four Jin Pills), Ba Wei Di Huang Wan (Eight Flavor Rehmannia Pill), and Jin Gang Wan (Diamond Pill), which can all be selected as needed. If there is yin deficiency without dampness or with excessive sweating, using Cang Zhu (Atractylodes) lightly is discouraged, as weakness is especially intolerant of exterior dispersing treatments and could further harm the yin. Dong Yuan considers Huang Bo (Phellodendron) as the primary medicine, supplemented by Huang Qi (Astragalus) and other tonics to treat various weakness conditions, without a fixed method. There can be cases with phlegm accumulation, excessive dampness and heat, a mix of damp and heat, or accompanying qi issues, in which case one should create a treatment plan according to the specific illness; this is also an excellent method for treating weakness.[38p]
    The "Zuan Yao" states: For damp-heat, the Jianbu pill should be supplemented with remedies that dry dampness and reduce fire, such as Huangbo, Huangkui, and Cangzhu. For damp-phlegm, add Cangzhu, Baizhu, Huangkui, and Huangbo to the Erchen Decoction, along with bamboo juice and ginger juice. For blood deficiency, add Cangzhu and Huangbo to the Four Herbal Compounds, and take the Xiangbui pill. For qi deficiency, add Cangzhu, Huangkui, and Huangbo to the Four Noble Herbs. Huangbo and Cangzhu are essential medicines for treating atrophy. Although the above prescriptions target different conditions, they all aim to reduce fire and clear dampness, which is the fundamental principle of treatment that remains consistent.

    Chen Wuze remarked: The human body is formed of skin, blood vessels, sinews, muscles, and bone marrow, with internal organs such as the liver, heart, spleen, lungs, and kidneys presiding over them. Yet, if one indulges in emotions, fatigue, or idleness, it can lead to the depletion of internal essence and blood, resulting in weak and feeble blood vessels and muscles, thus causing atrophy that resembles symptoms of soft wind syndrome. Soft wind syndrome occurs due to external factors like wind and cold, where the righteous qi clashes with the evil qi, causing swelling and pain, indicating a real pathogen. The atrophy arises from insufficient internal organs; however, if one does not overexert themselves, there will be no pain, indicating a deficiency of blood and qi.[39p]
    Treatment for Weakness

    When a person suffers from complete depletion of vital energy and blood, resulting in multiple ailments throughout the body, they may experience continuous headaches accompanied by bone pain or abdominal pain along with pain in all four limbs. These symptoms, often indicative of weakness, should primarily be treated by addressing the Yangming stomach fire. However, if the stomach fire is overly vigorous and the body, including the limbs, becomes emaciated, one cannot solely target the stomach meridian.

    The formula consists of three qian each of ginseng, astragalus, white peony root, poria, white mustard seed, and angelica, five qian each of job's tears and plantago seeds, one qian of chrysanthemum flowers, one liang each of ophiopogon and cooked rehmannia, and five fen each of dried tangerine peel and fermented wheat. Boil these in three large bowls of water until it reduces to one bowl, and take this as a dose.

    While one might ordinarily use bamboo leaf and gypsum decoction when Yangming fire is excessive, the choice to utilize ginseng, poria, astragalus, and cooked herbs as the primary ingredients is made to replenish vital energy and blood. The stomach fire is so strong that it has already scorched the vital energy and blood; further use of the Baihu decoction would temporarily relieve the fire toxicity, but it would inevitably lead to a severe depletion of bodily fluids. Thus, it is preferable to use a large dose of tonifying herbs for energy and blood, as adequate hydration will naturally quell the fire.[40p]
    In the middle of the formula, one should appropriately use ingredients such as Yuan Shen, Mai Dong, and Gan Ju, which are purely meant to quell the spreading fire of the Yang Ming. Moreover, with the addition of Dang Gui and similar herbs to nourish blood and support the source, this is quite effective. However, if the dosage of these herbs is small, it not only fails to benefit but may actually exacerbate the fire’s intensity; this is the brilliance of this major treatment method. Generally, the major treatment method should be applied at the onset of deficiency conditions with excess stomach fire; using large doses can turn defeat into victory. If one hesitates and is cautious initially, fearing to use large doses, and waits until the stomach qi has weakened before realizing the need for large doses, it will be too late.

    For cases of weakness where the individual cannot get out of bed year-round, has a glowing complexion, weak and powerless feet, and cannot take a step, this indicates an excess of Yang Ming fire. It is unnecessary to treat the legs directly; instead, calming the stomach fire will naturally strengthen the legs. If one does not calm the stomach fire and only uses nourishing yin herbs, an increase in food intake will further weaken the legs. The method should include three taels of Yuan Shen, one tael each of Mai Dong and Shu Di, three qian of Gan Ju Hua, and one qian each of Ren Shen and Tu Si Zi, boiled with several bowls of water to yield four bowls of decoction. Allowing for unrestricted consumption will gradually calm the stomach fire, and the legs will naturally regain strength. Thus, this approach treats the legs indirectly.

    It is essential to discern that not all weakness is due to Yang Ming deficiency; this distinction must not be overlooked.[41p]
    This condition cannot result in the patient getting out of bed, yet they can eat well. The patient feels weak and cannot stand. People think this is due to deficiency, but it is not. This is due to extreme cold in the kidneys leading to a boiling fire; it appears as deficiency yet is not. At the onset, it was indeed due to the blazing fire of the yangming, and if cooled down, the stomach fire would diminish, while the kidney water would dry up, leading to nighttime coughing and phlegm. During the day, the patient feels somewhat better, groaning on the bed, eating little and slowly, and feeling increasingly uncomfortable.

    The prescription should be one tael of Yuan Shen, three taels of Mai Dong, and two taels of cooked dihuang, prepared with water. If there is liver fire, add five qian of Bai Shao and prepare with water. Four doses may allow the patient to get out of bed. Subsequently, a large dose of Six Flavor Decoction should be prepared, adding one tael each of Mai Dong and cooked dihuang, one qian of Wu Wei, four qian of Shan Zhu Yu, three qian each of Shan Yao and Dan Pi, and two qian each of Ze Xie and Fu Ling, brewed with water.

    The brilliance of this formula lies in the use of Yuan Shen and Mai Dong to nourish the lung metal and eliminate the wandering fire in the heart; it also excels in using cooked dihuang to replenish kidney water, which ensures there is enough water and strengthens the stomach fire. When the lung metal naturally nourishes kidney water, the kidney water will be stored in the kidney palace and will not surge upward, thus resolving the cough without needing to suppress it.[42p]
    The weakness of the legs, unable to walk, is thought to be due to a deficiency of kidney essence, but that is not the case. The issue is that the qi is insufficient to function properly. The prescription is to use the Bu Zhong Yi Qi Decoction, adding three liang each of ginseng and ox knee, five liang of golden needle stone, and one liang of astragalus. After two doses, strength will return to the legs; after four doses, one should be able to walk. Ginseng, astragalus, and atractylodes are all supreme tonics for qi, while ox knee and golden needle stone are divine remedies for strengthening the legs, making this combination effective.

    As for those with atrophy, they must lie in bed for a long time and cannot suddenly rise; what is the reason? All the symptoms of atrophy belong to the yangming stomach fire. When this fire exhausts the kidney essence, the bones become empty and without nourishment, making standing impossible. However, if only treating the yangming, when will the marrow in the bones be replenished? It is difficult to regain strength in both legs. The method is to use one liang each of yuanshen and ophiopogon, two liang of rehmannia, and two qian of ox knee, decocting and taking it. The brilliance of this formula lies in not treating the yangming, but directly addressing the kidney channel to replenish its deficiency. When kidney essence is restored, the stomach fire will naturally subside. Moreover, the ophiopogon clears lung qi, and the ox knee strengthens the knees and shins; thus, using this prescription long-term can prevent the symptoms of atrophy.[43p]
    If one only uses gypsum and rhizome of anemarrhena to bring down the fire in the stomach, the fire will be lowered, and the kidney's water will further dry up. Then, what will replenish the bone marrow? It's no wonder that after years of treatment, one becomes increasingly fatigued. The symptoms of weakness and atrophy are due to the excess fire of the Yangming. If the kidney's water is insufficient to nourish, then the bones become hollow and cannot support the body.

    The prescription should include three taels of rehmannia, three taels of shan zhu yu, two taels of mai dong, boiled in water and taken. The essence of this formula lies in the fact that rehmannia and shan zhu yu together enrich the water, while rehmannia removes the floating fire, and mai dong nourishes the lung's Yin, allowing Yin to flourish and Yang to diminish, naturally extinguishing the fire’s flame. Once the flame is extinguished, the metal and water will regenerate, there will be fluids in the organs, and the bone marrow will be replenished. Can the legs still be incapable of walking?

    Regarding the prescription for weakness, use three taels of coix, three taels of rehmannia, one tael of mai dong, one qian of north wuwu herb, and five qian of niu xi, boiled in water and taken. The brilliance of this formula is that, with three taels of coix, rehmannia will not be overly damp, mai dong will not be excessively cold, and niu xi will not be too aggressive, allowing one to gain benefits from all three ingredients, enabling long-term use and achieving results.[44p]
    When atrophy (weizheng) persists without improvement, it is because the fire of the Yangming has completely consumed the water of the kidneys. However, how can one avoid death and continue to exist? Although the kidney water is dried up, the metal of the lungs still obtains the vital energy from the stomach to nourish it. When the lung metal has energy, it must flow down to generate kidney water. Despite the kidneys being shriveled, there will always be some moisture at night, and the kidney channel continually has vitality; hence, one survives and does not die.

    The prescription is to use half a pound of ophiopogon, one pound of cooked rehmannia, seven taels of rhemannia root, and one tael of schisandra, boiled in twenty bowls of water until six bowls remain. Take three bowls in the morning, two in the afternoon, and one at midnight for two consecutive days; one will surely be able to sit up.

    Then, change the prescription to eight taels of cooked rehmannia, three taels of rhemannia root, four taels of ophiopogon, three qian of north schisandra, four qian of cornelian cherry, and one tael of cattle’s knee; boil in ten bowls of water until two bowls remain, taking one bowl in the morning and one in the evening. After ten days, one will be able to walk, and after a month, one will return to normal.

    By greatly nourishing the water of the lungs and kidneys, the fire of the Yangming will cease to consume itself. The reason for atrophy lasting without death is that it stems from the moisture provided by the lungs, supported by the roots of the kidneys. If the kidney water had no roots, where would the night moisture needed for the lung metal arise from? I would simply see it as a withering. It is precisely because there are roots that one does not die.[45p]
    Therefore, using large doses of kidney tonics, one can recover from illness, as they have a foundation that can be salvaged. The treatment for atrophy also varies. The formula consists of one tael each of Yuanshen (Radix Ginseng) and Shanzhuyu (Cornus Fruit), three taels each of Shudi (Rehmanniae Radix) and Shashen (Adenophora Root), four taels of Maidong (Ophiopogon), five qian of Wuweizi (Schisandra Fruit), decoct in water for ingestion. In ten days, one can be up and about; I have personally tested this, and it is remarkably effective.

    For those with atrophy who cannot get out of bed and have become useless, the internal fire is intensely burning, having dried up the kidney water. If one does not replenish the kidneys and only aims to reduce the fire, there will be no hope of rejuvenation. Although treating atrophy often focuses on the Yangming (the stomach's fire), the stomach fire must certainly be reduced, while the kidney water must also be replenished.

    I now share an extraordinary formula that replenishes water amidst fire, reduces fire within water, harmonizes the stomach and kidneys, thus treating both conditions simultaneously. Naturally, bone marrow will increase, and burning heat will disperse; the atrophy will self-cure without direct treatment. This formula is named "Jiang Bu Dan" (Reinforcing and Reducing Pill): It consists of three qian of Ren Shen (Ginseng), one tael each of Shudi, Yuanshen, and Maidong, five qian each of Ganju Hua (Chrysanthemum), Shengdi (Raw Rehmannia), Shashen, and Digupi (Lycium Bark), and two qian of Cheqianzi (Plantago Seed); decoct in water for ingestion. This formula includes both replenishing and reducing ingredients, making it truly wondrous.[46p]
    When the fire of the stomach does not arise, it will not consume the yin in the kidneys; when the water of the kidneys is sufficient, it can control the heat in the stomach. The two interact and support each other, creating a remedy for atrophy—who can surpass this? When a person has weak legs and cannot stand, yet their appetite remains strong, if they resist hunger even slightly, their head and face will feel hot, and they may have persistent coughing—this is also a sign of atrophy, as the heat of the stomach fire rushes upwards to the lung metal. The lung metal is pressured by fire, unable to transmit the clear and purifying energy to the lower abdomen, causing kidney water to dry up, the marrow in the bones to diminish, thus they cannot stand. Meanwhile, the fire of the stomach burns on, allowing them to eat well yet still hunger; over time, the water will dry up and the marrow will be depleted, leading to death—must we not urgently drain the fire from the stomach? However, if we drain the fire without supplementing the water, the stomach fire will have nothing to restrain it, making it hard to cease. The formula for awakening atrophy is as follows: [ingredients] cooked rehmannia, yam, and original root, each one tael; ginseng, Angelica sinensis, and white peony root, each five qian; white mustard seed three qian, fermented wheat two qian; decoct and consume. After one dose, the fire decreases; after two doses, the fire retreats; after ten doses, there will be signs of recovery from atrophy, and thirty doses may achieve complete healing. This formula is remarkable as the sweet chrysanthemum flower serves as the monarch, draining the fire of the yangming without harming the qi of the stomach; the rest merely supplements kidney water to generate liver blood, strengthens the spleen qi, and alleviates phlegm. In essence, treating atrophy focuses on the yangming; draining yangming first, then assisting with other herbs, makes success easier.[47p]

    Section on Paralysis and Weakness

    Formula: Di Huang Yin Zi (by He Jian)

    This formula treats mutism, weakness of the kidneys, reversals of bodily functions, inability to speak, and paralysis of the feet.

    Ingredients:

    • Cooked Rehmannia
    • Morinda Root
    • Cornelian Cherry
    • Dendrobium
    • Processed Cistanche Wine
    • Prepared Aconite
    • Five Flavored Fruit
    • Cinnamon
    • White Poria
    • Ophiopogon
    • Sweetflag
    • Polygala

    All ingredients should be ground into a fine powder. The recommended dosage is three qian (a traditional weight unit) per serving, mixed with one and a half bowls of water, five slices of ginger, one jujube, and some mint, simmer until the volume reduces to around one-third. There is no specific timing for taking this remedy.[48p]
    Qingzao Decoction (same as Dongyuan)

    Treats atrophy caused by summer heat and dampness, leading to paralysis below the waist, inability to move, and improper walking with tilted feet.

    Ingredients:

    • Huang Lian (Coptis)
    • Jiu Huang Lian (wine-processed Coptis)
    • Chai Hu (Bupleurum) - each 1 fen
    • Mai Dong (Ophiopogon)
    • Zhu Ling (Polyporus)
    • Dang Gui (Angelica) - all 2 fen
    • Sheng Di (raw Rehmannia)
    • Zhi Gan Cao (honey-fried licorice)
    • Shen Qu (fermented wheat) - each 2 fen
    • Ren Shen (ginseng)
    • Fu Ling (Poria)
    • Sheng Ma (Anemone) - each 3 fen
    • Cang Zhu (Atractylodes) - 1 qian
    • Ju Pi (tangerine peel)
    • Bai Zhu (Atractylodes)
    • Ze Xie (Alisma) - each 5 fen
    • Huang Qi (Astragalus) - 1 qian 5 fen
    • Wu Wei Zi (Schisandra) - 9 seeds

    Preparation:
    Grind the ingredients to the size of millet. For each dose, use 1.5 taels of water, boil until reduced to 1 cup, strain, and take warm on an empty stomach.

    To modify this formula, reduce Chai Hu, Huang Lian, Zhu Ling, and Ze Xie, naming it Reduced Qingzao Decoction, used to treat bone atrophy.

    Qing Shu Yi Qi Decoction

    Treats atrophy due to damage to the stomach qi from heat.

    Ingredients:

    • Huang Qi (Astragalus)
    • Cang Zhu (Atractylodes, soaked and peeled) - each 1 qian 5 fen
    • Sheng Ma (Anemone) - 1 qian
    • Ren Shen (ginseng)
    • Bai Zhu (Atractylodes)
    • Ju Pi (tangerine peel)
    • Shen Qu (fermented wheat, fried)
    • Ze Xie (Alisma) - each 5 fen
    • Zhi Gan Cao (honey-fried licorice)
    • Jiu Huang Lian (wine-processed Coptis)
    • Dang Gui (Angelica)
    • Mai Dong (without heart)
    • Qing Pi (green tangerine peel without white)
    • Ge Gen (Pueraria) - each 3 fen
    • Wu Wei Zi (Schisandra) - 9 seeds

    Preparation:
    Grind the ingredients and prepare as a dose using 1.5 taels of water, boil until reduced to 1 cup, strain, and take warm, with food avoided for a while. If sweating is minimal, reduce Huang Qi to 5 fen.[49p]

    Treats blood and qi deficiency, weakness in both legs, inability to move, and prolonged bed rest. Remarkable effects.
    Ingredients: 1 pound of deer antler glue, 0.5 pounds of deer antler powder, 0.5 pounds of cooked rehmannia, 2 taels each of白术 (Atractylodes), and du zhong (Eucommia), 1 tael each of niu xi (Achyranthes), white poria, tu si zi (Cuscuta), ginseng, roasted tiger tibia fat, and roasted turtle shell fat.
